An exception is the sensor calibration plots\n(appendix E), which are unnumbered\nand are arranged in\nalphabetical\norder by mnemonic name within appendix E.\n\nThe first phase of the Modern Technology Rotor\nprogram, the Modern Rotor Aerodynamic\nLimits Survey,\nwas a flight test conducted by the United States Army\nAviation Engineering\nFlight Activity for NASA Ames\nResearch Center. The test was performed using a United\nStates Army UH-60A Black Hawk aircraft and the United\nStates Air Force HH-60A Night Hawk instrumented\nmain-rotor blade. The primary purpose of this test was to\ngather high-speed, steady-state,\nand maneuvering\ndata\nsuitable for correlation purposes with analytical prediction\ntools. All aspects of the data base, flight-test instrumentation, and test procedures are presented and analyzed.\nBecause of the high volume of data, only select data\npoints are presented here. However, access to the entire\ndata set is available upon request.\n\n1. INTRODUCTION\n\nMRALS\n\nAerodynamic\nLimits Survey (MRALS),\nconducted on a\nUH-60A Black Hawk, was divided into four sections:\nhigh-speed limits; maneuver limits; stability and control;\nand acoustics. The sensors included in the test are categorized as follows: rotor parameters, fuselage vibration,\naircraft state, and engine parameters.\nThe data accumulated from this first phase of the Modern Technology\nRotor Program reside at Ames Research Center and are\naccessible through two data-analysis/management\ncomputer programs, the Tilt Rotor ENgineering\nDatabase\nSystem (TRENDS) and the Data from Aeromechanics\nTest and Analytics-Management\nand Analysis Program\n(DATAMAP).\nA data survey is presented here covering a sample of\neach of the sensor types included in this test. The survey\nincludes both statistical and time-history\ndata plots and\nsummary tables. Data accuracy and data-base limitations\nare discussed. A data analysis section is included which\naddresses many of the phenomena found in the data.\nAppendixes provide reference information on the following: UH-60A aircraft physical characteristics\n(appendix\nA); flight cards (appendix B); Information File for\n\nthe\n\ninvolved\n\nAND EQUIPMENT\n\nthe use of two aircraft,\n\na UH-60A\n\nand a YO-3A, the AEFA ground station, and special\ninstrumentation.\nThe primary data were recorded on board\nthe UH-60A test aircraft; the NASA YO-3A Acoustic\nResearch Aircraft was used to obtain the in-flight acoustics data. The ground station was used to monitor flight\nloads, maintain test conditions, and provide preliminary\npostflight data processing. The special instrumentation\non\nthe UH-60A consisted of sensors measuring blade motion,\nrotor loads and vibration, control loads, and fuselage\nvibration.\nUH-60A\n\nThis report describes a flight test conducted in 1987\nby the United States Army Aviation Engineering Flight\nActivity (AEFA) at Edwards Air Force Base, California,\nfor the NASA Ames Research Center. The Modem Rotor\n\nDATAMAP\n(appendix C); sign conventions\n(appendix D); and sensor calibration information\n(appendix E).\nThe numbered tables and figures cited throughout\ntext appear after the main text, appendixes,\nand refer-\n\n2. INSTRUMENTATION\n\nBlack\n\nHawk\n\nThe UH-60A Black Hawk used in this test, tail number 23748, is shown in figure 1. The physical characteristics of the UH-60A are presented in appendix A. This discussion of the aircraft will cover the basic production\nqualities and the special instrumentation\ninstalled for this\ntest. The test equipment included for this test can be\ndivided into the following categories: fuselage, rotor\nsystem, and data system.\nThe aircraft was manned by a pilot, co-pilot, and\nflight-test engineer. During MRALS, the flight engineer\ncontrolled and verified the operation of the tape recorder,\nmaintained the desired aircraft longitudinal center of\ngravity (c.g.) trim using the ballast cart, monitored the\nstatus of the test condition, and maintained\nthe flight notes\non the flight cards.\nThe UH-60A flight-control\nsystem includes five\nmajor automatic subsystems:\nthe stability augmentation\nsystem (SAS); flight-path-stabilization\nsystem (FPS); trim\nsystem; stabilator control system; and pitch bias actuator\n(PBA). The SAS subsystem is a dual subsystem consisting of a digital (SAS 1) and an analog (SAS2) control. The\nSAS is designed to provide three-axis rate-damping,\npartial attitude retention, and limited turn coordination.\nThe\nFPS is designed to provide three-axis attitude-hold,\nairspeed-hold,\nand principal turn coordination.\nThe trim\nsystem is designed to provide stick-position-hold\nand\nforce-feel. The stabilator control system positions the\nstabilator as a function of airspeed and collective positions, and is designed to control the aircraft pitch attitude\nas a function of airspeed. The PBA was designed to insure\npositive static and dynamic longitudinal\nstability. It was\nfound to be of little benefit, however, and was disabled for\n\nthistest. oredetailed\nM\ndescriptions\noftheseontrol\nc\nsystems provided\nare\ninreference\n1.\nTheUH-60A equipped\nwas\nwithaballast shown\ncart,\ninfigure\n2,thattravels\nlongitudinally\ntocompensate\nfor\nfuelburn-off, maintaining\nthus\naconstant\naircraft\nlongitudinal .g.Additional\nc\nballast\nweight addedtthree\nwas\na\nlocations\ntoachieve desired coefficients.\nthe\nthrust\nFigure\n3shows ne\no ofthetwolocations thefuel\nover\ntanks\nbehind\ntheaftcabin\nbulkhead.\nThethirdsitewas\non\nthecabin\nfloortoeither ide\ns ofthe\nengineers During\nseat.\ntheacoustics atransmitter, infigure\ntest\nshown\n4,was\ninstalled\ninthenose\nofthe\naircraft\ntosend\ntheonce-permain-rotor-revolution contactor tothe\n(1/rev)\nsignal\nYO-3A\naircraft.\nInstrumentation\nsystem- pulse modulation\nA\ncode\n(PCM) ata-acquisition known\nd\nsystem,\nasthe\nhighcapacity,\norHi-Cap,\nsystem used uring\nwas d\nthisflight\ntest. he\nT system setupforthistestwith58wordsn\nwas\ni\nthemainframe,\nwithtwosubframes.\nThefirstsubframe\nwas levels eep, thesecond\n4\nd\nand\nwas16levels eep.\nd\nThis\nprovidedata\nd sampling\natnominal of517,\nrates\n129.25, 32.3\nand samples/sec. PCM used\nThe\nmap forthe\ntestispresented\nintable\n1,which\ndetailshelocations\nt\nof\nallsensors,\naswellastheirsample Filteringf the\nrates.\no\ndata provided\nwas\nasapart f the\no\nsignal\nconditioning.\nEach point asidentified\ntest\nw\nbyaunique called\nlabel\nacounter. counter\nThe\nisanumerical thathas\nseries\nthe\nflightnumber hundreds and\ninthe\nplace beginstzero\na\nfor\neach\nflight.Anexample\nofthisscheme becounter\nwould\n2208\nwhich\nistheeighthestpoint btained\nt\no\nonflight22.\nThiswas firstfieldtest f the\nthe\no\nHi-Cap\nsystem,\nshown\ninfigure\n5;allthingsonsidered,\nc\nit workedery\nv\nwell.The were\ndata\nrecorded\nbyusing\nanon-board,\n14-track,\nwideband\nFMtape\nrecorder;\nthedata also\nwere\ntelemetered groundtation\ntothe\ns\nwhereground was\na\ntape\nrecorded\nasbackup. ground was toprovide\nThe\ntape used\ndata\nforthesecondalfofflight20,counters\nh\n2015\nthrough021, fterhe\n2\na t on-board\nrecorder\nranoutoftape.\nAll instrumentation\nonboard\ntheaircraft given\nwas\ntwoseparate\nidentification\nlabels--mnemonicsitem\nand\ncodes. labels re\nBoth\na alphanumeric;mnemonics\nthe\ncontainptoeight haracters,\nu\nc\nwhereas item\nthe code\ncontains\nprecisely\nfourcharacters. types\nBoth\nofidentification\nlabels ere\nw required AEFAuses nemonics,\nsince\nm\nthe analysis\ndata\nprogram\nTRENDSsesither, the\nu e\nand\nprogram\nDATAMAP onlytheitem\nuses\ncode.\nThetwo\nanalysis\nprograms\narediscussed\ninsection.\n5\nThemnemonics\ngenerally\nareabbreviations\nof the\nsensor\nname.\nExamples\nofmnemonics\narePITCHATT,\npitchattitude;\nSTABLR,\nhorizontal\nstabilizer\nposition;\nand\nAZPS,\nvertical\nacceleration pilots\natthe\nstation.\nNot\nallmnemonics\nabbreviations\naresoobvious,\nhowever,\nas\nillustrated\nbyPAICB, hich\nw\nistheboom\nsystem\nstatic\npressure.\n\nItem\ncodes\nfallintoone of the following four sets:\none letter and three digits; two letters and two digits; three\nletters and one digit; and four letters. All four-letter item\ncodes are derived parameters (i.e., calculated, not\nmeasured) with the following three exceptions: BFAT,\nBFAR, and CART, which stand for blade tip and root\nflapwise accelerometers,\nand ballast cart position, respectively. The item codes use letters to denote sensor type or\naircraft component or both, and numbers to denote exact\nphysical location or sensor orientation. Examples of the\nfirst-letter notations are the following: "A" denotes an\naccelerometer;\n"B" denotes a sensor related to the\ninstrumented\nblade; "D" denotes a sensor that measures\nan aircraft state; "E" denotes an engine-related\nparameter;\n"H" denotes an altitude measurement;\n"M" denotes a\nsensor related to the rotor; "R" is a miscellaneous\ngrouping; "T" denotes a temperature reading; and "V" denotes a\nvelocity sensor. The second and third letters provide\nfurther identification\nof the sensor type. Examples of the\nuse of numbers are BN50, which denotes the bladenormal stress at 50% radius, and ET01, which denotes the\nengine turbine temperature of engine No. 1. All aircraftstate parameters, such as control positions, body attitudes,\nrates, and accelerations,\nuse the numeric code of zero for\nlongitudinal,\norientations.\n\n1 for lateral, 2 for yaw, and 3 for horizontal\nTable 2 summarizes\nthe item-code structure.\n\nFuselageThe fuselage instrumentation\nincludes\nwhat are collectively known as aircraft-state\nparameters\nand airframe vibration measurements.\nThe aircraft-state\nparameters include fuselage attitudes, rates, and angular\nand linear accelerations.\nThese are housed on a pallet on\nthe aft cabin bulkhead, as shown in figure 5. Control-stick\npositions, engine data, main-rotor speed, and both mainand tail-rotor contactors are also included in the aircraftstate measurement\nlist. The aircraft is equipped with an\ninstrumentation\nboom that monitors static and dynamic\npressure, outside air temperature,\nand angles of attack and\nside slip. A low-airspeed\nsensor was installed (fig. 6) in\norder to obtain accurate velocity measurements\nwhere the\npitot static system did not function. Many of the aircraft\ncontrol-system\ncomponents\nwere instrumented\nfor this\ntest, including the output motion of the three primary\nservos (fig. 7), along with SAS outputs and control mixer\ninput signals. The tail rotor had only minimal instrumentation, which included tail-rotor shaft torque, by means of\nslip rings at the intermediate\ngear box (fig. 8) and a tailrotor once-per-rev\ncontactor (fig. 9). The aircraft-state\nparameters comprise three categories: aircraft parameters\n(table 3), test condition (table 4), and engine parameters\n(table 5).\nTable 6 presents the mnemonics,\nitem codes, and\norientations\nof the fuselage vibration sensors. The locations of the accelerometers\nwere selected to match those\nused on an airframe\n\nshake test conducted\n\nby Sikorsky\n\nAircraft,n support the\ni\nof\n\nNASA Langley Design\nAnalysis Methods for Vibrations (DAMVIBS)\nprogram\n(ref. 2). The precise physical locations of the fuselage\naccelerometers\nare given in table 7. The accelerometers\nwere sampled so as to provide data up to 20 harmonics,\nalthough the processed data are filtered such that only the\nfirst 10 harmonics are included in the data base.\nRotor systemThe rotor-system\ninstrumentation\nis\ndivided into blade loads, control loads, and hub measurements. The blade was instrumented\nwith strain gauges to\nmeasure normal, edgewise, and total stresses, as well as\nblade-root and tip normal accelerations.\nIncluded with the\nblade-load measurement\nis the pitch-link load. The control\nloads primarily include nonrotating hardware, whereas the\nhub sensors consist of orthogonal accelerations,\nblade\nmotions, and shaft parameters. Table 8 presents a\ncomplete sensor list of the rotor-system\nparameters.\nThe instrumented\nblade used for the MRALS was\nobtained from the USAAF Night Hawk program. The\nblade is only slightly modified from the production blade\nby the addition of instrumentation\nwiring laid down in the\ntroughs cut into the skin of both the top and bottom\nsurfaces. The aerodynamic\ncontour of the blade is interrupted to some extent, because the room-temperature\nvulcanizing (RTV) compound used to cover the wires did\nnot harden to a uniform surface. The instrumentation\nembedded in the Night Hawk blade included four normal,\nthree edgewise, three total, and two tip-cap strain gauges.\nThe two tip gauges were disconnected,\nfor MRALS,\nand were replaced by two accelerometers,\none normal and\none edgewise (fig. 10). The tip-normal accelerometer\nwas\nmatched with a root accelerometer\nmounted on the\noutboard section of the hub arm (fig. 11).\nThe tip-normal accelerometer\nfailed early in the test;\nbecause it was the more important of the two tip sensors,\nthe edgewise tip accelerometer\nwas used to replace it.\nThis quick fix initially caused havoc with postflight data\nprocessing, which was not flexible enough to handle\nsensor swapping. However, after modification\nthis too\nwas remedied.\nThe pitch link that connects the instrumented\nblade to\nthe swash plate was instrumented\nwith strain gauges to\nmeasure the axial control loads. The stationary rotor control links were instrumented\nwith strain gauges to measure\naxial loads. These values were monitored during flight.\nThe hub instrumentation\ngroup consists of\naccelerometers,\nstrain gauges, and motion pots. The hub\nwas instrumented\nwith three orthogonal accelerometers\n(fig. 12). The blade-motion\nhardware (fig. 13) was developed for the Rotorcraft Systems Integration Simulator\n(RSIS) flight test, conducted by AEFA in 1981-1982\n(ref. 3). The special hardware was required because of the\nunusual hinge arrangement\nof the hub. The blade-motion\nin flap, feather, and lead-lag is allowed by an elastomeric\n\nbearing in each arm of the hub. Proper measurement\nwith\nthis hardware requires a complex and meticulous calibration, the theory of which is outlined in appendix F. The\nshaft strain gauges are shown in figure 14.\nDerived parametersA group of derived parameters\nhas been included, along with the measured parameters,\nin\nthe stored data base. Table 9 presents the mnemonics,\nitem codes, units, and descriptions\nof these derived\nparameters. The exact equations\nderived parameters are available\nYO-3A\n\nAcoustic\n\nResearch\n\nused to compute the\nas a part of the data base.\n\nAircraft\n\nAcoustic data of the UH-60 were taken during MRALS\nby the YO-3A Acoustic Research Aircraft (fig. i 5). The\nAcoustic Research Aircraft is a specially instrumented\nversion of the low-speed observation\naircraft manufactured for the military by the Lockheed Aircraft Corporation, which is used as a flying microphone\nplatform for\nthe study of rotorcraft noise. The YO-3A Acoustic\nResearch Aircraft is equipped with a special instrumentation package which includes three 0.5-in. microphones,\none on each wing tip and one atop the vertical tail; gainadjustable microphone power supplies; an instrumentation\nboom; a radio link with the test helicopter,\nwhich carries\nthe main-rotor contactor signal; an IRIG-B time-code\nreceiver; and a 14-track FM tape recorder.\nThe YO-3A is powered by a highly modified\nContinental engine (210 hp), which is equipped with a\nthree-bladed,\nwide-chord wooden propeller. The engine\n\nis\n\nequipped with a very effective muffler which, combined\nwith the low-tip-speed\npropeller, results in a very quiet\naircraft. A thorough discussion of this aircraft is presented\nin reference 4.\n\n3. TEST\n\nDESCRIPTION\n\nThe conduct of the test is divided into four general categories: performance\nlimits, maneuver limits, dynamic\nstability, and acoustics. All test points were partially\ndefined by the nondimensional\nvalue of thrust coefficient\nover sigma (CT/C), and the referred rotor speed (Nr/vr0t).\nThe remainder of the test-point definitions were set by the\nrequirements\nunique to each of the four categories.\nDetailed descriptions\nof the required piloting techniques\nused to acquire the various test-point types are presented\nin reference 1.\nThe ground station was used during each of the four\ntest categories testings to establish the required test\naltitude and rotor speed. In the event of telemetry (TM)\nfailure (which did occur) or extended site testing (which\nalso occurred), the on-board flight-test engineer used a\n\nhand-held,\nportable\ncalculator\ntomake\nthenecessary\ncalculations.\nTwoseparate\nsupport were\ntests conducted\ninsupport\nofMRALS:\nadynamic\nshake oftheinstrumented\ntest\nNightHawk\nblade,\nconducted theflighttest; nd\nbefore\na a\nfuselage testwhich conducted\nshake\nwas\nconcurrently\nwiththe\nflighttest.\nPerformance\n\nLimits\n\nThe objective of the performance-limits\ntest element was\nto measure the increase in rotor loads and fuselage vibration as a function of airspeed and correlate that with\nanalytical predictions\nand ground test. This portion of the\ntest matrix was designed to obtain performance\ndata from\nall sensors from hover to Vne at CT/O values of 0.08,\n0.09, and 0.10. Table 10 presents the test points obtained\nduring this portion of the test.\nThe data from hover to V h were obtained in level\nflight at approximately\n5,000 ft pressure altitude. The\nspeeds from V h out to Vne were achieved while in a powered descent. Additional test points were obtained at\nseveral speeds below V h while at maximum power. The\nlow-airspeed\nsensor was used to establish airspeed below\n25 KIAS; above this speed the ships system was used.\nThe SAS systems were disengaged\nfor these test points,\nexcept at the high-speed end, where they were required.\nManeuver\n\nLimits\n\nThe test objective for the maneuver-limits\nelement of\nthe program was to obtain high-g data in a constant steady\nmaneuver; that is to say that airspeed, pitch attitude, roll\nattitude, pitch rate, pitch angular acceleration,\nand\ng loading, were to be held constant. All other means of\nachieving high-g loads require that some of these be\ncontinually varying. It is felt that constant maneuvers\nsimplify the correlation effort with many of the\ncomprehensive\nrotorcraft codes.\nThis portion of the test matrix obtained vibration and\nblade-loads\ndata encountered\nin high-speed wind-up turns\nat two gross weight conditions. Table 11 presents the test\npoints obtained during this portion of the test. The\nmaneuver limits data was originally to be obtained at the\nsame altitude as the performance\ndata; however, because\nof temporary\naltitude restrictions placed on the aircraft\nduring the time of testing, the target altitude was raised to\n9,000 ft. This made it impossible to obtain data at\nCT/t_ = 0.08. The maneuver limits tests provide aircraft\nresponse data at high-load and high-speed conditions\nwhich can then be compared with level and descending\nflight results.\nThese points were obtained by flying to an initial\naltitude of over 11,000 ft, establishing airspeed, nosing\n\n4\n\nthe aircraft over and beginning the wind-up turn. The\nbank angle was varied to 37 \xc2\xb0 , 48 \xc2\xb0 , 55 \xc2\xb0 , 60 \xc2\xb0, and 65 \xc2\xb0 and\nheld for 5 sec of steady data. Several bank angles were\nusually attained during each descent through the target\naltitude, while the tape ran continuously.\nTelemetry was\nmonitored to assess the quality of each bank-angle condition and to monitor load buildup. Rotor and control\nendurance limits were exceeded at many of the higher\nspeed points.\nDynamic\n\nStability\n\nThe objective of the dynamic stability test was to\nquantitatively\nmeasure the UH-60\'s unaugmented,\nrigidbody dynamic response to various discrete control inputs.\nDynamic stability, a secondary goal of MRALS, was\nincluded primarily to provide some data with which to\nevaluate the capabilities of comprehensive\nanalysis computer codes in modeling dynamic stability. An additional\ninterest in obtaining these data was the individual blade\ncontrol (IBC) concept, which is discussed in detail in\nsection 7. The control inputs consisted of doublets and\nsinusoidal control sweeps The dynamic stability tests\nwere performed from trim conditions at two airspeeds:\n60 and 140 knots calibrated, and the sinosoidal sweeps\nwere conducted at hover and 108 KIAS (table 12). The\ncontrol inputs consisted of longitudinal,\nlateral, directional, and collective doublets of approximately\n+1 in.\nfrom the trim positions. The doublet inputs were designed\nto have a total duration of 1.0 sec, 0.5 sec/pulse, before\nreturning to trim. The control was then held for approximately 7 sec, or until corrective control action was\nrequired. To ensure that only the unaugmented\naircraft\nresponse was measured, both stability augmentation\nsystems and the flight-path stabilization system were\ndisengaged.\nAcoustics\nThe acoustic data from MRALS was gathered to\nserve as a baseline for the more encompassing\ntests to be\nconducted during the second phase of the Modern Technology Rotor program. The test matrix that was flown\nduring phase 1, as shown in table 13, was therefore\nrelatively modest. The air-to-air acoustics data were\nobtained with the YO-3A Acoustic Research Aircraft\nflying formation with the UH-60A, as depicted in figure 16. Three formations, trail, left, and right (fig. 17),\nwere flown during this portion of the flight-test program.\nThe trail formation consists of the UH-60 flying 1.5 rotor\ndiameters behind the YO-3A with its rotor hub in the\nhorizontal plane of the tail-mounted\nmicrophone. The left\nand right formations are mirror images of each other, with\nthe UH-60 at 30 \xc2\xb0 elevation above and behind the\n\nBlade and control loads time-histories\nare stored at\nrespective\nwing-tip-mounted\nmicrophone\natadistance\nof\n1.5\nrotor\ndiameters.\nTheproper\naircraft\nseparation\nwas\nthe full rate provided by the on-board instrumentation\nobtained\nbyusing\nanoptical\nrangeinder, hich\nf\nw\nwas\nsystem, but vibration and aircraft-state\nsensors have been\noperated\nbyone\nofthe\npilots. hile\nW inthetrailformation, filtered and decimated. The standard package includes the mean,\nmaximum, minimum, and standard deviation of each\n\nTes_\n\nTwo support tests were conducted as an integral part\nof MRALS, a blade-shake test, and a fuselage-shake\ntest.\nThe blade-shake\ntest was conducted using the instrumented Night Hawk blade prior to commencement\nof\nflight testing. The test involved applying forces to the root\nend of the blade with a shaker, while the blade was sus-\n\nsensor for each counter. The per-rev package includes the\naverage vibratory, average steady, 95th-percentile\nvibratory, maximum vibratory, and steady value at maximum\nvibratory. The standard package applies the statistical\nequations to all of the data for each sensor and to each\ncounter. The per-rev package, however, first performs the\nstatistics on the data from each revolution of each sensor\n\npended with bungee cords in a vertical orientation. The\ntests produced data on mode shapes and natural frequencies, which are discussed in more detail in section 6 of\n\nin a counter, then averages those results to produce the\nvalues for that counter. Detailed definitions of these\nstatistical data are discussed in reference 10.\n\nthis report and in reference 5.\nThe fuselage-shake\ntest was conducted by Sikorsky\nunder a modification\nto the NASA Langley DAMVIBS\nprogram. This test involved loading the test fuselage to\nmodel the flight aircraft including ballast, fuel, instrumentation, and crew. It should be noted that the test fuselage\nwas not that of the flight-test vehicle. Final test results and\ncorrelation with NASTRAN\npredictions are presented in\nreferences 2 and 6.\n\nThe first 15 harmonics are computed and stored for a\nselect list of parameters and are then included in the data\nbase, accessible for analysis. The maximum, minimum,\nand mean for each revolution, computed in the per-rev\nstatistics package, are added to the data base for selected\nsensors. This information is presented for each counter as\n\n4. DATA\n\nData Processing\n\nPROCESSING\n\nAND ACCESS\n\na histogram and a revolution history (as opposed\ntime-history)\nplot. The narrative data documents\n\nflights, as to time, place, events, personnel, and test\npoints, and are an integral part of the TRENDS data base.\n\nThe data from MRALS\nThe goal of processing the flight data was to produce\na data base in the proper format for use with the two data\nanalysis programs, TRENDS (Tilt Rotor Engineering\nDatabase System) and DATAMAP\n(Data from Aeromechanics Test and Analytics-Management\nand Analysis\nPackage) (refs. 7-9). The data are stored in what has\nbecome known as the TRENDS format. This actually\nconsists of several different formats, depending on the\ntypes of data. Data are referenced to a specific sensor\n(referred to as a mnemonic or item code) and test point\n(referred to as a counter).\nData-Base\n\nContents\n\nThe data base for the UH-60 consists of timehistories, statistical summaries,\nharmonics, loads, and\nnarratives. Each of these is discussed below.\n\nto a\nthe\n\nfollowed\n\na circuitous\n\nroute\n\n(fig. 18) from the flight tape to the data base. The first\nstep in the process converted the data from the flight tape\ninto the standard AEFA compressor\nformat. These formatted data were then reprocessed\ninto the TRENDS data\nformat as statistics and time-histories.\nThe on-site engineering evaluation team reviewed all data by using\nTRENDS, critiquing for data quality and consistency.\nAssuming that the quality checks demonstrated\ngood data,\neach test point was evaluated for its premier data. The\npremier data were defined to be that section of stable data\nthat best matched the desired test condition. This section\nof the data was called the "time slice." Backup tapes of\nthe statistical files were made for transfer of the data to\nAmes. The time-history\nslices were then copied to tape\nfrom the AEFA formatted data, for transfer to Ames by\nusing the CUTNSAVE\n\nroutine.\n\nAt Ames, the data-transfer\ntapes were reprocessed\nusing the FILLER routine to produce TRENDS formatted\ndata for permanent\nstorage. The processing with FILLER\nat Ames produced statistical files and time-histories\nfrom\nthe time-sliced data. Statistical data of the full test points,\nfrom the backup tapes produced at AEFA, were included\nin the data base at Ames also.\nA part of the data processing was the manual entry\ninto the data base, through use of the BASKER routine, of\nthe related narrative summaries. The narrative summaries\ndocument the flight log, flight descriptions,\ndescriptions.\n\nan optical-disk data retrieval system. Access to the data\nfrom MRALS is obtained in one of two ways using the\ntwo data access programs TRENDS and DATAMAP.\nThe\nprogram TRENDS provides access of time-history data to\nDATAMAP\nfrom inside of TRENDS, or the data files can\nbe accessed directly from DATAMAP.\nThe TRENDS\nprogram provides access to all of these various data types,\nwhereas DATAMAP\nonly provides access to the timehistory data base.\n\nand maneuver\n5. DATA\n\nSURVEY\n\nData Reviews\nThe data were processed during the conduct of the\ntest so that they could be reviewed for data quality and\nconsistency\nin near-real time. This included looking for\nspikes, band edge, time errors, dead transducers, and\nmisscalings.\nTwo special programs were used in evaluating the data processing quality, in addition to TRENDS.\nTo ensure parity, a program called MERGER was used to\ncompare the statistical data in the AEFA format with that\nin TRENDS. A routine called HAZEL was used to\ncompare the statistical data from the baseline and current\nflights housekeeping\npoints. The results, although not\nperfect, are much improved over what they would have\nbeen without this effort.\nThe data review was followed by the selection of the\nprime data, or time-slice, with reprocessing\nof those data\nfrom the AEFA to TRENDS format for inclusion in the\npermanent data base at Ames. Each test point consisted of\nmore data than were required for storage in the data base.\nThe purpose of the time-slice was to store only those data\nthat were closest to the desired test condition.\nThe process of selecting the time-slice involved a\nroutine in TRENDS called Normalize. Select parameter\ntime-histories\nwere plotted which were first subtracted by\nthe statistical average and then divided by a predetermined allowable deviation value. An example is shown in\nfigure 19. The 5 sec of data that appeared to be the steadiest, and within the allowable band of+l was selected for\ninclusion in the final data base. This technique was not\nused for transient maneuvers,\nfor these test points were\nself-defining\nand the data were selected accordingly.\nThe\nstatistical files in the final data base contain two subsets,\nthe full test-point data and the selected prime-data-timeslice statistics. When accessing data in TRENDS, the\nprime data statistics are the default values.\nData Access\nThe MRALS\nResearch\n\nCenter\'s\n\ndata base is resident\ncomputing\n\nfacility\n\nat the Ames\nwhere it is stored on\n\nThis section presents samples of every major instrumentation category for a select subset of test points. The\ndata are presented as statistical plots versus advance ratio,\nand also as time-history\nand azimuthal plots. The entire\ndata base resides on the Ames Research Center computing\nfacility. Statistical data sets of select sensors and derived\nparameters are presented for the level-flight\nspeed sweeps,\nand for the high-g and dynamic stability maneuvers.\nSelected time-histories\nare presented to highlight the\nspecific changes shown in the statistical plots. The cycleaveraged time-history data presented in this report have\nbeen averaged over several consecutive rotor revolutions.\nThe consecutive cycles used were those whose control\ninputs and aircraft states were the closest to steady state of\nthe available time-histories.\nThe data presented in the\nSpeed Sweep subsection have been averaged over 15 consecutive cycles; the data presented in the high-g maneuver\nsection have been averaged over 8 revolutions.\nData Anomalies\nIn the process of reviewing the data obtained from the\nPhase I flight test, several observations\nwere made\nregarding data anomalies. All of these anomalies have\nbeen removed from the user accessible data base. The\nanomalies are of the following varieties: excessive spiking; band edge; incorrect scaling bias and scaling factor;\npot slippage; static drift; cross-labeling\nof several\nparameters;\nand excessive noise.\nWhere possible, the encountered spikes have been\nremoved from the data base, using a routine in the\nTRENDS data maintenance\nprogram. The routine takes\nthe two end points that bound the spike and replace the\nspike with their average.\nThe data found to contain band-edge have been\nflagged and removed from the available data base. As a\nresult, for a given flight, certain sensors may not be\navailable for all counters.\nDuring postflight processing, the conversion from\nPCM counts to engineering units was occasionally\n\nassigned\nthe\n\nwrong slope or offset. This has been rectified\nby adjusting the stored bias or scaling factor resident in\nthe data base. The procedure for this is to adjust the bias\nby the offset found with the R-Cai value for the affected\nflight, or to adjust the scaling factor by the offset found in\nthe average oscillatory values for the affected flight\ncompared with a comparable\ntest point on one or more\nunaffected flights. These corrections have been quite rare,\noccurring only on sensors BE01, BE50, and BN70.\nSlippage of the motion pots used on the blade-motion\nhardware caused errors in the flap, feather, and lead-lag\nmeasurements.\nAt present, these have not been corrected,\nbut they have been removed from the accessible data base.\nTwo aircraft-state\nvariables, roll rate and yaw rate,\nwere found to be cross-labeled,\nand that problem has been\nrectified. Aircraft angular accelerometer\nmeasurements\nwere excessively\nnoisy during much of the flight program\nand have been removed from much of the data base. All\ndata that have been found to be excessively\nnoisy have\nbeen filtered, where possible, and removed, where\nfiltering was not possible.\nThe aircraft was instrumented\nwith two tail-rotor\ntorque gauges, for historically\nthis has been a troublesome\nparameter to maintain, principally because of the high\nwear rate of the tail-rotor slip rings. On most of the\nflights, this parameter gave incorrect results. A correlation\nof these data with previous test data has been performed.\nFigure 20 presents the composite curve that gives the best\nestimate of what tail-rotor torque should be for a speed\nsweep.\n\npitch and yaw rates. The physical dimensions of the\ninstrumentation\nboom sensors are given in appendix A.\nThese measurements\nmust be adjusted when used in\nanalysis to compensate\nfor the unwanted additional\ncomponents.\nSpeed Sweep\nFigures 21 through 28 present the statistical mean\nvalues of control positions, main-rotor torque, coefficients\nof thrust and power, and advancing-tip\nMach number for\nall test points of the speed-sweep\nsubset, at all three aircraft gross weight configurations.\nThese plots present the\neffects of the gross weight change and the consistency\nof\nthe test points. Figures 29 through 38 present aircraft-state\ndata taken at CT/t_ = 0.09 only. Figures 39 through 47\npresent blade, control, and pitch-link loads. They each\nconsist of two plots: the top one presents the mean value\nand the bottom one presents the average oscillatory\nvalues. The figures presenting statistical values are followed\nby figures of time-history data. Figures 48 through 51 present normal blade bending at 50, 60, and 70% radius, and\npush-rod load versus rotor azimuth, respectively.\nA list of\nadvance ratio, angle of attack, angle of side slip, and\nengine torque for these counters is presented in table 14.\nFigures 52 through 55 present the average oscillatory\nvalues of the vertical accelerometers\nat the pilots seat,\nmain-rotor hub, vertical tail, and right aft cabin, locations.\nFigures 56 and 57 present the vertical and lateral\naccelerometer\naverage oscillatory data for the right\nforward cabin station.\n\nSensor Limitations\nHigh-g\nEach of the sensors\n\nincluded\n\nin MRALS\n\nTurn\n\nhave\n\ncapability limitations that restrict their application. The\nmore subtle of these will be discussed here. Applicable\ndimensions are provided in appendix A.\nThe LASSIE low-airspeed\ndata system (VX03,\nVY03, VZ03) measures the longitudinal,\nlateral, and\nvertical velocity of the air mass under the rotor. It was\ncalibrated in the low-speed flight regime only, out to 50\nknots. Any attempt to use this sensor in any other flight\nregime will yield incorrect results. In addition, the data\nstored in the data base are the raw values, not the\ncalibrated values. The calibrations were used in the\ncomputation of the true airspeed (VTRU) only.\nThe aircraft-state\nmeasurements\nrelative to the center\nof gravity (c.g.) were, of necessity, not measured at the\nc.g. Their exact locations are given in appendix A. These\nmeasurements\nmust be adjusted when used in analysis, in\norder to compensate\nfor the physical offset.\nThe aircraft angle of attack and sideslip vanes measure the local angles, not the angles at the c.g. Hence, they\ninclude moment-arm\ncomponents\nthat arise as a result of\n\nManeuver\n\ndata were recorded\n\nwith the aircraft\n\nballasted for CT/t_ = 0.09 and 0.10 in level flight at the\ntest pressure altitude of 9,000 ft. This section presents\ndata of selected sensors from the 0.09 CT/_ configured\naircraft. Each plot\ncontains the relevant level flight loads and loads obtained\nin both left and right turns at the indicated g loading. The\nvalues presented here are not the statistical values resident\non the data base at Ames. INVESTIGATIONS\n\nThis section discusses various phenomena\nobserved\nin the data survey just presented. A summary discussion\nof a gust-alleviation\nstudy known as individual blade\ncontrol (IBC) is also presented. The following discussions\nwill often refer to the figures presented in the preceding\nsection.\nPerformance\n\nand High-Speed\n\nLimits\n\nOne of the principal interests in conducting this test\nwas that of the power train and structural limits encountered in high-speed flight. The particular structural limits\nof interest are the rotor-control\nand blade loads. The data\npresented in figures 24, 25, 28, 37, and 38, in section 5,\nshow the increase in the power train loads as speed is\nincreased. The component limit is defined for this test as\nthat speed at which the slope of the curve increases. The\nparticular curve of interest, that is, average or oscillatory,\ndepends on the sensor. The oscillatory curve is used to\ndefine the limit for structural hardware, such as rotorcontrol loads. The average curve is used for power train\ncomponents.\nThis definition of the term "limit" does not\ninvolve component\nlife, as is usually the case.\nThe data presented in section 5 (figs. 39 through 47)\nshow the increase in these loads as speed is increased, for\nCT/C = 0.09. Figure 39 shows pitch-link load, both average and oscillatory,\nversus advance ratio (p.). The mean\nloading is bell-shaped,\nwith the peak occurring around\nI = 0.18. The high-speed end, 0.35 and greater, is\na\n\nrelatively\n\nflat and, not coincidentally,\n\nthat portion\n\nof the\n\nspeed sweep conducted in a powered descent. The V h for\nthis data set resulted in an advance ratio of 0.38.\nThe plot of average oscillatory load is characterized\nby a slight positive slope out to _t = 0.3 where the curve\nslope increases sharply. The curve flattens out slightly just\npast the point of maximum level flight, where the aircraft\nbegan its powered descent. The curve then increases in\nslope to a value greater than that before the aircraft began\nits powered descent.\nThe corresponding\ntime-history plots for pitch-link\nload are presented in figure 51. The plots are presented\nwith rotor azimuth on the abscissa and with a conventional orientation of zero over the tail boom. Each plot can\nbe divided into the following four quadrants: first, 0 \xc2\xb0 90\xc2\xb0; second, 90 \xc2\xb0 - 180\xc2\xb0; third, 180 \xc2\xb0 - 270\xc2\xb0; fourth, 270 \xc2\xb0 360 \xc2\xb0 . The statistical summary data for the counters\npresent in these time-history plots are listed in table 14.\nThe loads approach zero at 60 \xc2\xb0 and 150 \xc2\xb0 azimuth,\nand reach a maximum negative value at 215 \xc2\xb0 and 300 \xc2\xb0\nazimuth at an advance ratio of 0.096 (counter 1708). The\nnegative peak is in the fourth quadrant and just exceeds\n1,000 ft.lb. The smallest values at this speed are\napproximately\none tenth the peak value.\nAs the speed increases to an advance ratio of 0.197\n(counter 1704), the zero approach in the first quadrant has\nbecome a slightly positive peak and has moved from 60 \xc2\xb0\nto 45 \xc2\xb0 . The zero approach at 150 \xc2\xb0 has disappeared\naltogether. The negative peak at 215 \xc2\xb0 has increased in value\nand shifted to 200 \xc2\xb0 . The second negative peak has\ndecreased in magnitude but has not shifted azimuthally.\nAt an advance ratio of 0.314 (counter 1717), the first\nquadrant positive peak has moved from 45 \xc2\xb0 to 35 \xc2\xb0 with no\nincrease in value, and the negative peak at 200 \xc2\xb0 has\nmoved to 160 \xc2\xb0 with a nearly 50% increase in value. The\nnegative peak in the fourth quadrant has shifted to the\nthird quadrant, to 255 \xc2\xb0 , and has increased to more than its\noriginal value at the slowest speed presented.\nAt an advance ratio of 0.395 (counter 3016), the\namplitudes have continued to increase and the peaks have\ncontinued to shift. The positive peak in the first azimuthal\nquadrant has continued its shift to 20". The large negative\npeak in the second quadrant has continued to grow in\nmagnitude and has rotated to 150 \xc2\xb0 . rightforward and aft-cabin floor, vertical tail and main-rotor\n\n57\n\nhub vertical sensors, and the right-forward\ncabin lateral\nsensor. The data include steady-state dives and climbs at\nconstant power, and show several trends that are of interest. The first is as expected; vibratory load increases\nexponentially\nwith airspeed. These loads are thought to be\n\n10\n\ncaused by the rotor high-speed phenomena of compressibility and dynamic stall. The increase in vibratory load at\nthe transitional advance ratios of 0.05 to 0.15 are also seen\nin the data. This is caused by rotor-wake interference.\nThe\ndata from the climbs and dives generally fall on top of the\nlevel-flight data. This indicates that the angle of attack of\nthe aircraft has a small influence on vibration levels.\nFinally, near hover the data show a fair amount of scatter.\nThe reason for this phenomenon,\ndiscussed further later in\nthis section, is unknown at this time.\nA harmonic analysis was also performed and saved in\nthe data base for the 18 accelerometers.\nHarmonic data are\nuseful in helping to identify sources of vibratory excitation. Figures 107 through 114 show a few examples of\nthis type of data. The data include the 4th, 8th, and 12th\nharmonics plotted versus advance ratio for the pilot floor,\nvertical tail, right-forward\ncabin floor, and the vertical hub\naccelerometers.\nThe three fuselage accelerometer\nplots\nshow increasing vibratory levels for all harmonics with\nincreasing advance ratio. However, the 4/rev harmonic of\nthe main-rotor hub is at a minimum at these advance\nratios. This points to a different source of vibratory excitation for the 4/rev component of the main-rotor hub than\nfor the fuselage. It is also interesting to note that the two\nvibratory levels at the low advance ratios (near hover)\nmentioned above, are also visible in the 4/rev harmonic\ncontent of all the accelerometers\npresented here. These\ndata suggest that the different vibratory levels at the low\nadvance ratios are related to a 4/rev phenomenon.\nDynamic\n\nStability\n\nThe dynamic stability tests were conducted to obtain\nhigh-quality flight-test data that could be used for simulation validation, preliminary control-system\ndesign, and\nparameter identification\nof six-degree-of-freedom\n(DOF)\nrigid-body dynamics. The input profile selected for these\ntests was the doublet, as described in section 3. The\ndoublet profile was chosen to excite the high-frequency\n(short-period)\ndynamics of the helicopter,\nwhile maintaining a reasonable range of aircraft body attitudes. Limiting\nthe aircraft excursions from trim allows the use of linear\nanalysis\nreduced\n\ntechniques with reasonable confidence. It also\nthe risk of an unscheduled\n"E-Ticket"\nride.\n\nAs seen in figures 88(a) and 88(b) the helicopter\nchange in attitude caused by the 60-knot longitudinal\ndoublet was less than +2 \xc2\xb0 in all axes, followed by divergence. The divergence initially began in pitch and was\nfollowed immediately\nby roll and yaw axes divergence.\nThis divergence was undoubtedly\ncaused by the phugoid\nmode, which is unstable at these flight conditions for the\nunaugmented\nUH-60 helicopter.\nFor the 140-knot pedal doublet, the deviations from\ntrim attitude caused by the input were less than +10 \xc2\xb0 in all\n\naxes,\nasshown figures\nin\n\n93(a) and 93(b). Although there\nwere initially much greater forces and displacements at\n140 knots than at 60 knots, the aircraft diverged more\nslowly because the phugoid mode is much less unstable at\nthe higher airspeed.\nAnother rigid-body\nmode is readily observed in the\nyaw-rate response to the pedal doublet\nure 94(b). The mode evident is clearly\n\nshown in figthe Dutch roll\n\nmode and is stable. Analysis of the yaw-rate response\nprovides a rough estimate of the Dutch roll mode characteristics. The mode is described approximately\nby the\nroots _ = -0.20 sec :t: 1.63i rad/sec (COn 1.64 rad/sec,\n=\n= 0.122). A perturbation analysis was performed, prior\nto the flight testing, using the Gen Hel Simulation program (ref. 11), for purposes of comparison.\nThis lateral\ndecoupled solution predicted a Dutch roll root of\n= -0.22 sec 5: 1.47i rad/sec (tOn = 1.49 rad/sec,\n= 0.148), which agrees very well with that estimated\nfrom the flight-test data.\nTo investigate the consistency\nof the flight-test data,\ncomparisons\nwere made of attitudes and rates. The two\ntypes of comparisons\nthat were made are shown in\nfigure 115 for the 60-knot longitudinal\ndoublet and in\nfigure 116 for the 140-knot pedal doublet. Figure 115(a)\nshows the time-derivative\nof the measured pitch attitude\n(d0/d0 compared with the estimated d0/dt based on other\nflight-test measurements and calculated from the Euler\nrate equation:\nd0/dt = q cos t_ - r sin\nwhere q and r are the angular body rates in pitch and yaw,\nrespectively, and t_ is the instantaneous aircraft roll attitude. The two curves show excellent agreement, with a\nvery slight amount of bias evident between the two\ncurves.\n\nThe comparison of"measured"\nand estimated d_/dt is\nshown in figure 115(b), with the estimated value\ncalculated from\nd_/dt = p + tan 0(r cos _ + q sin t_)\nwhere p is the roll rate. The two curves are virtually identical, with no apparent phase or magnitude shift. Figure 116(b) shows the same comparison\nfor the pedal\ninput, and a similar correlation is evident.\nFigure 116(a) shows the comparison of measured and\nestimated dWdt with the estimated value calculated from\ndv/dt = sec 0(r cos t_ + q sin t_)\nwhere \xc2\xa5 is the aircraft heading angle. Again, it is seen that\nthe two responses exhibit nearly identical behavior.\nAlthough these curves demonstrate\nthat the aircraft\nattitudes and body rates are all consistent, they do not\nform the basis of an exhaustive effort to determine all\nscale and bias errors present in the flight-test data. It is\nrecommended that these data be more closely examined\n\nusing state-estimation\ntechniques, or other kinematic\nanalysis tools prior to detailed dynamic investigations.\nIndividual\n\nBlade Control\n\nThe individual blade control (IBC) investigation\nwas\nconducted as part of a cooperative\nagreement with the\nMassachusetts\nInstitute of Technology.\nThis section will\ngive a basic description of the IBC concept and sample\nresults obtained from this flight test. A more complete\nanalysis and description\nof this investigation\nmay be\nfound in reference 12.\nIn a true IBC scheme,\n\neach blade would\n\nbe controlled\n\nindependently\nthrough use of individual, high-bandwidth\nactuators located in the rotating system. The controller\nwould consist of several subsystems and be designed in a\nmodal fashion where each subsystem would be fine-tuned\nto a particular frequency application. The controller would\nuse feedback signals from sensors mounted on each blade\nto determine the required control inputs. The true IBC\nsystem is therefore very flexible, and allows the control of\ndynamic phenomena\nthat occur at any frequency,\nregardless of the rotor rotational speed.\nHowever, it is also possible to use a conventional\nswashplate to control certain multiples of the rotor\nfrequency. In a four-bladed\nrotor system, for example, the\n0P to 1P and 3P to 5P harmonics can be controlled using a\nswashplate, thus allowing a type of"pseudo"\nindividual\nblade control. This pseudo IBC can then be used to\ncontrol many of the undesirable dynamic effects inherent\nto a four-bladed\nhelicopter, since they occur at the rotor\nharmonics listed above. Examples of these undesirable\neffects include gust response (0P to 1P) and vibration (IP\nand 4P). The IBC investigation\nis presently focused on the\nlow-frequency\ngust alleviation system. This system would\nrequire feedback of the 1P blade flapping acceleration,\nrate, and displacement,\nand a controller optimized for the\n0P to 1P range.\nThe purpose of this flight test was simply to demonstrate that blade-mounted\nsensors (accelerometers)\ncould\npotentially provide accurate feedback signals to a\ncontroller. This flight test was entirely an open-loop\nexperiment,\nwith no controller or control-system\ninterface\ninstalled on the aircraft.\nTwo miniature accelerometers\nwere placed on the\ninstrumented\nNight Hawk rotor blade as shown in\nfigure 117. The design range of the root accelerometer\nwas _+5g and the range of the tip accelerometer\nwas\n_+250 g. The accelerometers\nwere mounted along the blade\nfeathering axis, with their sensitive axis approximately\nparallel to the main-rotor shaft. To account for blade-pitch\nchanges, the accelerometers were mounted on the blade at\nan angle that would represent an average collective\nposition in flight.\n\n11\n\nFigures18 and 119 show the time-history and\n1\nfrequency response of the root and tip accelerometers\nat\n80 knots. The root accelerometer\ndisplays more highfrequency content than the tip accelerometer.\nThis is\nlikely a result of the combination\nof a more sensitive\ninstrument (15 mV/g at the root vs 1 mV/g at the tip), and\nmuch lower overall acceleration\nlevels at the root.\nIn order to use these accelerations\nas feedback signals\nto a gust-alleviation\ncontroller, the flapping position and\nacceleration\n(15 and 15") must be determined.\nThe most\nelementary model of blade motion assumes a totally rigid\nblade. Only steady and IP rigid-flapping\nmotion remain in\nthis simple approach;\n15" and 15may then be easily calculated using the blade accelerometer\ninformation by\n15"= [rra t - arr t ] / [e(r t - rr)]\n15= [(e - rr)a t - (e - rt )a r ] / (_2e(rt\n\n- rr)]\n\nwhere\nar\nat\nrr\nrt\ne\n\nroot acceleration\ntip acceleration\nradial location\n\nradial location of tip accelerometer\nblade-hinge\noffset\nmust be filtered\n\nto the\n\nfrequency range of interest, which in this case is approximately 1P (4.3 Hz) before they can be used. Figure 120\nshows the relative root- and tip-accelerometer\nresponse at\n80 knots for an average of four rotor revolutions.\nThese\ndata were processed with a 5-Hz convolution\nfilter.\nComparison\nof the accelerometer\nresponses reveals that\nthere is a significant phase difference between the root\nand the tip signals. The tip response apparently leads the\nroot response by approximately\n42 \xc2\xb0 of rotor azimuth at\nthe 80-knot flight condition. Analysis of other flight\nconditions shows that various degrees of phase shift exist\nat all airspeeds and rotor Ioadings. Figure 121 shows the\nblade flapping based on the accelerometer\nmeasurements,\nincluding the phase shift.\nThe existence of the phase difference between the\nroot and tip accelerations\nis not completely unexpected\nwhen one considers that the blade is not rigid and that it\nbehaves elastically in flight. However, this phase difference does cause a problem when computing 15and 15"\nfrom the simple equations above, which do not consider\nany elastic motion. The two accelerometer\nsignals would\nhave to be phase-aligned\nin order to correctly calculate 15\nand 15" for the rigid-flapping\ncase. However, shifting the\nphase of the signals will complicate any controller design.\nSince the phase differences are not constant with airspeed,\nadditional inputs to the controller are required, and gains\nmust be scheduled for airspeed.\n\n12\n\nphase problem would be eliminated. However, this\narrangement\nwould only work for rigid-flapping\nestimates\nused in gust alleviation or handling-qualities-type\nimprovements.\nAny consideration\nof vibration reduction\nwould require a minimum of four sensors at various radial\nlocations in order to estimate the first flatwise bending\nmode.\nAnother major problem is the rigid-blade model\nitself. A completely rigid-blade model is far too restricting, and does not physically represent the blade dynamics\nin flight. It is, therefore, recommended\nthat to more\naccurately model the flapping motion, at least the first\nelastic bending mode be considered in the blade\ndynamics.\nBlade-Shake\n\nof root accelerometer\n\nThe blade accelerations\n\nA possible alternative to the current root-tip sensor\nlocations, which may help reduce the phase-shift problem\ncaused by blade bending, would be to move the tip\naccelerometer\ninboard. By placing the two accelerometers\nclose together and near the root of the blade, bending\neffects would be greatly reduced, and the subsequent\n\nTest\n\nA part of the UH-60 phase 1 test documentation\nincludes a modal analysis shake test, preformed during the\nsummer of 1986, of the Night Hawk instrumented\nblade.\nThe shake test was conducted to accurately document the\ndynamic characteristics\nof the instrumented\nblade. The\nresults have been compared with the blade as modeled for\nthe prediction codes that are used in correlation studies\nwith the flight-test data. The blade-shake\ntest was\nconducted to simulate a free-free boundary condition.\nThis was accomplished\nby suspending the blade vertically\nfrom the root end by means of bungee chords. A shaker\nattached to the blade by a thin stinger at the blade root\nwas anchored to the support structure.\nThe results of the test are reported in reference 5;\nthey include the frequencies,\ndamping, and mode shapes\nof the first five flapping modes, two chordwise modes,\nand two torsion modes. Table 18 shows the frequencies\nand damping measured during the test. Figure 122\npresents the first and second flapwise mode shapes\nobtained from the test.\nLow-Speed\n\nData Scatter\n\nA recurrent feature found in nearly all of the speed\nsweep plots, figures 21 through 47, is a split in the data at\nthe low-speed end. This split is present in all three CT/O\ndata sets and has been a subject of much study during the\ndata evaluation phase of this program. Figure 123 presents\npitch-link load time-history data plotted versus main-rotor\nazimuth, with data from both sides of the data split. It is\nreadily seen that the wave forms of the two subsets are\n\ndistinctlyifferent,\nd\nthefrequency\ncontent\nisdifferent,\nand\nthere\nappears\ntobeaphasehiftaswell.Select\ns\nstatistical\naircraft-state fortheseounters\nvalues\nc\narepresented\nin\ntable19. here been\nT\nhas\nnoacceptable\nphysical\nexplanationforthisoccurrence, noevidence beenound\nand\nhas f\ntoindicatemalfunction\na\noftheinstrumentation\nsystem.\nBecause\nthedata\ncannot\nbediscounted, have\nthey\nbeen\nretained\ninthedata\nbase, provideninteresting\nand\na\narea\nforfurther\nstudy.\n7. PREDICTIONS\n\nOne of the primary purposes of MRALS was to\nobtain quality data for use in correlating with predictions\nfrom several comprehensive\nanalytical computer codes,\nnotably the Comprehensive\nAnalytical Modeling of\nRotorcraft Aerodynamics\nand Dynamics (CAMRAD)\n(refs. 13 and 14)and C-81 (refs. 15 and 16). NASA also\nhas a modified analysis code originally developed by\nSikorsky Aircraft called Gen Hel (ref. 1 I), for which a\nBlack Hawk model is available.\nA workshop with industry participation\nwas\nconducted for the purpose of introducing\nthe MRALS data\nbase. As a part of the workshop, manufacturers\nwere\ncontracted to predict pitch-link loads using prediction\ntools of their choice for comparison with the high-speed\ntest points. Predictions were also made by NASA using\nCAMRAD. The results of the prediction efforts are\npresented in figure 124. With the exception of company\nNo. 2, the results were not especially accurate. It should\nbe noted that the test points being modeled are high speed,\nand were obtained in a dive. This introduces many\nvariables, which, if improperly accounted for, could\nadversely affect the correlation.\nAn inhouse correlation study of CAMRAD and the\nMRALS data has been undertaken (ref. 17). The effort\nfocuses on structural blade loads; an example of the\nresults is presented in figure 125.\n\n8. CONCLUDING\n\nREMARKS\n\nIt is the intent of this report that it serve not only as a\ndata survey, but also as the reference source for all\nmatters relating to the Modern Rotor Aerodynamic\nLimits\nSurvey (MRALS). As such, in addition to the presentation\nof sample data, this report contains detailed descriptions\nof the instrumentation,\ntest hardware, and test procedures\nused during the test, as well as brief descriptions of the\npertinent data formats and data analysis tools. Six\n\nappendixes\nhave been included in the report so as to\ncomplete the documentation\non the first test phase.\nThe sample data presented here include examples of\nall the various sensor types for a speed-sweep\nfrom hover\nto Vne at a CT/C of 0.09. The data are presented as plots\nof statistical averages versus advance ratio, and azimuthal\nand time-history plots. The data base has been rigorously\nreviewed for errors, and all detected errors have been\nremoved. The data have been reviewed from the perspective of various technical disciplines, including dynamic\nstability, vibration, and maneuver and high-speed loads.\nThe more prominent aerodynamic\nand dynamic phenomena found in the data have been discussed. In addition, a\ngust-alleviation\nconcept, individual blade control, was\nreviewed, and the details of a blade-shake\ntest are\nsummarized.\nThe data base currently resides on the Ames Research\nCenter computer complex. Access to the data can be\nobtained in many ways. Among them are interactive use\nof either TRENDS or DATAMAP\non the host computer\nthrough a remote modem, transfer of selected data subsets\nin the TRENDS format via digital tape, or transfer of\ndigital tapes containing harmonic tables stored in a\nNASA-specified\nformat.\nThe data obtained from MRALS are currently being\nused in correlation studies with several comprehensive\nrotorcraft codes, including CAMRAD, C-81, Gen Hel,\nand CAMRAD/JA.\nAn industry/academia/government\nworkshop was held in June 1988 to introduce the data\nbase to potential users. The workshop involved hands-on\nsessions with TRENDS and DATAMAP,\na review of\nindustry predictions of pitch-link loads, and a review of\nthe flight-test program.\nThis first phase is the beginning of a comprehensive\nprogram to document the physical, aerodynamic,\nand\ndynamic characteristics\nof the UH-60. It is to be followed\nby a second phase which includes extensive airloads,\nmuch more thorough blade loads, blade vibration, hub\nimpedance, control loads, and a more thorough fuselage\nvibration survey. A third phase is planned for an entry\ninto the National Fullscale Aerodynamic\nComplex\n(NFAC), that will complement\nthe flight data with tunnel\ntesting. A rigorous fuselage-shake\ntest of the flight vehicle\nis planned to follow the specific in-flight vibration test\nmatrix. The goal of the program is to provide a single\ncomplete, accurate, documented data base for use in\nunderstanding\nbasic helicopter phenomena,\nand for\ncorrelation efforts with advanced predictive codes.\n\nAmes Research\n\nCenter\n\nNational Aeronautics\nand Space Administration\nMoffett Field, CA 94035-1000\nJanuary\n\n1, 1992\n\n13\n\nAPPENDIX\n\nA.\n\nUH-60A\n\nAIRCRAFT\n\nThe dimensions and pertinent characteristics\nof the\nBlack Hawk test aircraft are presented here. Each flight\ncard contains a short summary of the flight, including\nflight number, flight date, test director, pilots, flight time,\nand counter range. This is followed by a list of the run\nnumbers (or counter numbers), the coded description of\n\nLEAD-LAG\n\nCal point, blade lag motion\n\neach test point, the duration of data for each test point, the\nstart time of each maneuver, and the data types available.\nEach counter is labeled with a code that identifies the\n\n60 KIASB,.08CTS,LEVEL\n\nThe flight cards present\n\na synopsis\n\nof each test flight,\n\ntest condition. The code is designed to make maximum\nuse of a feature in TRENDS which allows searches of the\ncounter\n\nlabels.\n\nThe result of such a search is a collection\n\nof test points that have in common the element searched\nfor (e.g., hover points, or RCALS, or housekeeping\npoints). The\nfile contains related sets of sensor item codes that are\norganized by their physical location, and that are given\nfour-character\ngroup names. Each group can be a one-,\ntwo-, or three-dimensional\narray. The third dimension is\nlimited to only two values.\nThe information file is divided into two sets of\ninformation.\nThe first sets equivalences\nthat relate item\ncodes with codes used in DATAMAP\nfor derivation\nequations. The first line, for example, equates the item\ncode MRZI with the internal code MRAZ, and sets\n82.63 \xc2\xb0 as the location of the instrumented\nblade When the\nMRZ1 blipper is triggered. All azimuthal plots generated\nneed this information to properly phase the rotating\nparameters. The word end is used to terminate this set.\nThe second set follows immediately\nafter the first. It\ncontains groups of sensors that are physically related. A\ngroup has a four-character\nname and includes item codes,\nlabels, and physical location information.\nEach group name is followed by a narrative description of that sensor set. This description is included on any\nMRAZ\nTRAZ\nTASK\nOATM\nSTAT\n\nMRZ1 82.63/\nMRZ2/\nVTRU/\nT 100/\nH001/\n\nUH-60/1\n\nplot produced using this group name. The next line\nidentifies the azimuthal offset of that sensor group with\nthe main-rotor once-per-rev\ncontactor. The next two lines\nare the labels applied to the first two dimensions\nof the\narray. These are followed by the physical locations of the\nsensors and the orientation of the first entrant, for the\nfirst-array dimension. If this is a two- or threedimensional array, the information\nfor the second-array\ndimension follows. Next is a four-character\ncode unique\nto the type of sensors included in the group. If the group is\na three-dimensional\narray, these codes are followed by the\norientation of the third dimension.\nIn the information\nfile, the item codes are presented\nlast and in the reverse of the order just discussed; that is,\nthe third dimension is varied first, then after a slash the\nsecond dimension is incremented and the third dimension\nis again varied. When the second dimension has been\ncompletely\nvaried, a double slash denotes that the first\ndimension is incremented.\nThe other two dimensions\nare\nthen varied as before. Each group information section is\nterminated with the word END. A more thorough explanation of the structure of the information\nfile can be found in\nreference 7.\n\nBENDING,\n\nUH-60/1\n\n0.10, 0.50, 0.70//\nBLBB//\nBE01/BE50/BE70//\nEND\nBENDING,\n\nS2VZ VERTICAL\nBUTT LINE\nINCHES\nCENTER LINE\n-35.5, -31.0,\nFUSELAGE\nINCHES\nFORWARD\n\n0.50, 0.60, 0.70//\nBLBB//\nBR50/BR60/BR70//\nEND\n\nNORMAL\n\nDATAMAP\n\nO.10, 0.50, 0.60, 0.70//\nBLBB//\nBN01/BN50/BN60/BN70//\nEND\n\nNBRB BLADE REAR BENDING,\nFRACTN OF RADIUS\nR/RADIUS\nBLADE ROOT\n\nNBNB BLADE\n\nFOR\n\nFRACTN OF RADIUS\nR/RADIUS\nBLADE ROOT\n\nMTOR RQ 10/\nMFLP BH01 82.63/\nMFTH BH02 82.63/\nEND\n\nNBEB BLADE EDGEWISE\nFRACTN OF RADIUS\nR/RADIUS\nBLADE ROOT\n\nFILE\n\nUH-60/1\n\nFUSELAGE\n\nVIBRATION,\n\nUH-60/!\n\n0.0, 31.0, 35.5//\nSTATION\n\n253.0, 295.0, 702.2//\nFSZV//\nNULL/AF04/NULL/AF02/NULL//\nAF07/NULL/NULL/NULL/AF06//\nAF 10/NULL/NULI_JNULLIAF09//\nNULL/NU LL/AF 12/NULL/NULL//\nEND\nS2VY LATERAL\nFUSELAGE\nFUSELAGE\nSTATION\nINCHES\nFORWARD\n\nVIBRATION,\n\nUH-60/1\n\n37\npI_IIB(>EI\'.qNGAGE\nP\n\nEH..Ai\'_KNOT\n\nFiLl\n\n253.0, 295.0, 398.0, 702.2//\nBUTT LINE\nINCHES\nCENTER LINE\n\nBUTT LINE\nINCHES\nCENTER LINE\n-83.5, 31.0, 83.5//\nFUSELAGE\nSTATION\nINCHES\nFORWARD\n\n-31.0, 0.0, 31.0, 35.5//\nFSYV//\nAF03/NULL/AF01/NULL//\nNULL/NULL/NULL/AF05//\nNULL/NULL/NULL/AF08//\nNULL/AF ! used in the test are presented\nhere. blade-motion\nhardware developed for the Black\nHawk aircraft has inherent interdependency\nof its measurement of blade flapping, feather, and lead-lag. The\nhardware produces three signal outputs that are crosscoupled, requiring a calibration to acquire the 10 coefficients of kinematic motion. SPONSORING/MONITORINGAGENCY NAME(S)ANDADDRESS(ES)\n\n10.\n\nSPONSORING/MONITORING\nAGENCY\n\nNational\nAeronautics\nand Space\nWashington,\nDC 20546-0001\n\n11.\n\nSUPPLEMENTARY\n\nSubject\n13.\n\n--\n\nCategory\n\nABSTRACT\n\nAdministration\n\nNASA TM-4446\n\nJ. Cross, Ames Research Center, MS 237-5, Moffett Field, CA 94035-1000;\n(415) 604-6571\n\nDISTRIBUTION/AVAILABILITY\n\nUnclassified\n\nNUMBER\n\nNOTES\n\nPoint of Contact:\n\n12a.\n\nREPORT\n\n(Maximum\n\nSTATEMENT\n\n12b.\n\nDISTRIBUTION\n\nCODE\n\nUnlimited\n01\n200\n\nwords)\n\nThe first phase of the Modem Technology Rotor program, the Modem Rotor Aerodynamic Limits Survey, was\na flight test conducted by the United States Army Aviation Engineering Flight Activity for NASAAmes Research\nCenter. The test was performed using a United States Army UH-60A Black Hawk aircraft and the United States Air\nForce HH-60ANight Hawk instrumented main-rotor blade. The primary purpose of this test was to gather high-speed,\nsteady-state, and maneuvering data suitable for correlation purposes with analytical prediction tools. All aspects of\nthe data base, flight-test instrumentation, and test procedures are presented and analyzed. Because of the high volume\nof data, only select data points are presented here. However, access to the entire data set is available upon request.\n\n14.\n\nSUBJECT\n\nTERMS\n15.\n\nUH-60, Rotor loads, Fuselage\n\nNUMBER\n\nOF\n\nPAGES\n\n270\n\nvibration\n16.\n\nPRICE\n\nCODE\n\nA12\n17.\n\nSECURITY\nOF REPORT\n\nCLASSIFICATION\n\nUnclassified\nNSN\n\n18.\n\nSECURITY\nOF THIS\n\nCLASSIFICATION\nPAGE\n\n19.\n\nSECURITY\nOF\n\nCLASSIFICATION\n\n20.\n\nLIMITATION\n\nOF\n\nABSTRACT\n\nABSTRACT\n\nUnclassified\n\n7540-01-280-5500\nStandard\nGPO\n\n583-676/19027\n\nPrescribed\n29e-\n\n102\n\nForm\nby\n\nANSI\n\n298\nStd\n\n(Rev.\n2\'39-18\n\n2-89)\n\n'
